class="MuiTypography-root MuiTypography-body2 ent-css-10xi6sq"><span>Published <!-- -->January 31, 2021. </span></p></div><div class="ent-tss-8gyp5s-postedTimeCommentsWrapper"></div><div class="ent-tss-71r2e1-mainImageWrapper"><div class="ent-tss-55ybit-imageContainer"><img loading="eager" id="ent-blog-image" class="ent-tss-32jtuo-previewCover-image" src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/700x700/kela_28-1741680590530.jpg" alt="a pie chart showing the percentage of initial access for sale" width="680" height="383"><button class="MuiButtonBase-root MuiIconButton-root MuiIconButton-sizeMedium ent-tss-yjupvi-expandIcon ent-tss-45-expandIcon-ref ent-css-1yxmbwk" tabindex="0" type="button"><svg class="MuiSvgIcon-root MuiSvgIcon-fontSizeMedium ent-css-vubbuv" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewbox="0 0 24 24" data-testid="OpenInFullIcon"><path d="M21 11V3h-8l3.29 3.29-10 10L3 13v8h8l-3.29-3.29 10-10z"></path></svg></button></div></div></div><div class="ent-tss-g7g134-blogContentWrapper"><div class="ent-tss-o5kd66-enContentEditor en-content"><div id="ent-blog-content" class="ent-tss-16bdfpw-editable" data-slate-editor="true" data-slate-node="value" contenteditable="false" zindex="-1" style="position:relative;outline:none;white-space:pre-wrap;word-wrap:break-word"><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Multiple initial network accesses continue to appear for sale in underground forums every day, partially becoming an initial entry point for ransomware operators. Following KELAs analysis of</span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://kelacyber.com/kelas-100-over-100-september2020-in-network-access-sales/" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">initial access brokers activities in September 2020</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">, weve assessed the listings of network access from all of Q4 2020.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Weve shared some of the major takeaways below:</span></span></span></p><ul data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-1jjvw86-listDisc"><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">KELA traced almost 250 initial network accesses listed for sale in Q4 2020. The cumulative price requested for all accesses surpasses $1.2 million</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">. On average, we observed around 80 accesses offered for sale in each month of Q4 2020.</span></span></span></li><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Out of these network access listings,</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">KELA found that at least 14% were noted as sold by actors.</span></strong></span></span></li><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">As the overall month-to-month number is lower than in September (108 accesses),</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">KELA identified a growing trend of accesses being sold in private conversations rather than publicly in forums, likely the cause for the slight decline.</span></strong></span></span></li><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">While establishing a list of the most expensive accesses and the TTPs of their sellers, KELA discovered that the attack surface is constantly expanding, with initial access brokers offering new access types.</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Meanwhile, RDP- and VPN-based accesses, as well as vulnerabilities (allowing to run code using a specific flaw and potentially enabling actors to pivot further within the targeted environment), constitute the majority of the offers.</span></span></span></li></ul><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-55ybit-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20680%20350'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="the info sheet for initial accesses for sale" width="680" height="350" data-lazy-src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x350/111024x527-1739857597303.png"><noscript><img style="width:100% !important" loading="lazy" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x350/111024x527-1739857597303.png" alt="the info sheet for initial accesses for sale" width="680" height="350"></noscript><button class="MuiButtonBase-root MuiIconButton-root MuiIconButton-sizeMedium ent-tss-yjupvi-expandIcon ent-tss-45-expandIcon-ref ent-css-1yxmbwk" tabindex="0" type="button"><svg class="MuiSvgIcon-root MuiSvgIcon-fontSizeMedium ent-css-vubbuv" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewbox="0 0 24 24" data-testid="OpenInFullIcon"><path d="M21 11V3h-8l3.29 3.29-10 10L3 13v8h8l-3.29-3.29 10-10z"></path></svg></button></div></div> <div data-slate-spacer="true" style="height:0;color:transparent;outline:none;position:absolute"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0"></span></span></span></div></div><h2 class="ent-tss-7785c4-h2" color="inherit" id="supply-q-4-2020"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Supply - Q4 2020</span></span></span></h2><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">KELA observed 242 accesses on sale in Q4 2020 on three underground forums  offered for a sum of $1,209,880*.[1] Its important to note that 24% of offers didnt specify the price. As well explain more later on, many accesses are being traded in private conversations, meaning this sum does not reflect the higher actual revenue of the initial access market.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">The average price for access has grown from $4,960 in September to $6,684 in Q4 2020, while the median price lowered from $2,000 to $1,500. This means that while high prices for selected offers significantly influence the average price, a typical access on sale still costs around $1,500-2,000. For such a sum, threat actors usually offer domain admin type of access to medium-sized companies with hundreds of employees.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div 11V3h-8l3.29 3.29-10 10L3 13v8h8l-3.29-3.29 10-10z"></path></svg></button></div></div> <div data-slate-spacer="true" style="height:0;color:transparent;outline:none;position:absolute"><span class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:center"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">Typical accesses offered for $1500-2000</span></em></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">KELA can confirm that at least 14% of initial network accesses were noted as sold by the actors selling them  amounting to a sum of $133,900.</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">This percentage has lowered since September (23%), but this metric depends only on brokers and their will to specify sold accesses or not. Therefore, it doesnt indicate that their conversion rate suddenly drastically changed; more likely, new actors who dont mark successful sales emerged on the market.</span></span></span></p><h2 class="ent-tss-7785c4-h2" color="inherit" id="operations-going-quiet"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Operations Going Quiet</span></span></span></h2><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">An average number of accesses on sale for one month in Q4 is 25% less than in September 2020.</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">However, it doesnt necessarily mean that initial access brokers suspended their activity. More possibly, they simply moved part of the deals in private conversations with middlemen or ransomware affiliates, in an effort to avoid detection from researchers.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">This hypothesis is based on the fact that many initial access brokers commonly write to contact them privately in order to receive information about other accesses that theyre willing to sell. While such behavior always existed, there is a more recent trend that emerged these past couple of months  brokers often offer a bunch of accesses in one thread and request from potential buyers to contact them privately to get the whole list. Some of them are looking for one buyer and state that theyre ready to work for a percentage, most likely meaning a share from the amount gained in a successful ransomware attack.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" accesses. The left post offers 70 Citrix accesses from Tier 1 countries, while the right posts author claims to have constant supply of domain admin accesses that he is ready to sell to one buyer.</span></em></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">In Q4 2020, the core of the most active initial access brokers consisted only of around 10 actors.</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">However, it doesnt mean that only their offers are valid and pose a significant threat. It seems that some prominent brokers moved their operations into private long-term deals with known threat actors. Some of these buyers can be actors that have dedicated threads on forums with titles such as Ill buy accesses, Buy networks, Buy Network Access to Corporations. Occasionally, such brokers in quiet-mode come back with pricey and valuable offers that they probably didnt succeed to sell.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">For example, a well-known actor who was quite active during the summer almost completely stopped sharing his offers publicly during September and October. However, his one offer posted in October 2020 was access to a government body for a price of $35,000 (see in Some Notable (and Pricey) Examples segment of this post). The next access he posted was only in December 2020  and it was a valuable offer as well  access to a US school district estimated at $20,000.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Another notorious actor made a comeback in late October after almost four months of radio-silence. He was the top actor offering the most expensive accesses in Q4, ranging from $30,000 to $135,000 (see in Some Notable (and Pricy) Examples segment of this post).</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">We can speculate that the actors who found regular buyers during the first months of their activity didnt succeed in selling some pricey accesses to them. Therefore, they went on a forum to try their luck with other users publicly. There are also other reasons for less accesses offered by the same actors  such as a time off (one of the actors was not active on the forum for several months), but we can only hypothesize regarding this matter.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" network accessmarket is bigger than what we are observing in public conversations happening on underground forums. To understand the real scope of threats, its necessary to keep tracking notorious initial access brokers and their TTPs, engage with them regularly, and identify new types of threats that may emerge.</span></strong></span></span></p><h2 class="ent-tss-7785c4-h2" color="inherit" id="further-diversification-of-accesses-on-sale-rmm-and-network-monitoring-solutions"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Further Diversification of Accesses on Sale: RMM and Network Monitoring Solutions</span></span></span></h2><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Every organizations attack surface is constantly expanding  initial access brokers are finding new attack vectors and ways to supply access to buyers. In some cases, they provide different types of accesses and even suggest making some malicious actions on behalf of the buyer, making their business more customer-oriented.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-55ybit-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20680%20474'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="A threat actor offers different access data-lazy-src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x474/9new1024x713-1739857977342.png"><noscript><img style="width:100% !important" loading="lazy" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x474/9new1024x713-1739857977342.png" alt="A threat actor offers different access privileges and types to compromise network of one organization." title="A threat actor offers different access privileges and types to compromise network of one organization." width="680" height="474"></noscript><button class="MuiButtonBase-root MuiIconButton-root MuiIconButton-sizeMedium ent-tss-yjupvi-expandIcon ent-tss-45-expandIcon-ref ent-css-1yxmbwk" tabindex="0" type="button"><svg class="MuiSvgIcon-root MuiSvgIcon-fontSizeMedium ent-css-vubbuv" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewbox="0 0 24 24" data-testid="OpenInFullIcon"><path d="M21 11V3h-8l3.29 3.29-10 10L3 13v8h8l-3.29-3.29 10-10z"></path></svg></button></div></div> <div data-slate-spacer="true" style="height:0;color:transparent;outline:none;position:absolute"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0"></span></span></span></div></div><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:center"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">A threat actor offers different access privileges and types to compromise network of one organization.</span></em></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">The most common offer is RDP- and VPN-based access, an RCE vulnerability, and access to various Citrix products (virtualization software, networking products and so on).</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">RDP- and VPN-based access, as well as access to different software, is usually provided in the form of valid credentials. After accessing a compromised machine via such access, an intruder can move laterally and eventually can succeed in stealing sensitive information, executing commands and delivering malware. The RCE vulnerability type of initial access is usually limited to the ability to run code using a specific vulnerability, which allows actors to pivot further within the targeted environment.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Such RDP- and VPN-based accesses, as well as RCE flaws and access to other popular products, constitute 45% of the accesses on sale, making them a more traditional type of access.</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">It is important to know that the actors specify a type of access only in half of the cases, while in the rest of the offers they just point out a level of privileges (admin or user, local or domain)  or simply give no details</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">.</span></strong></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-55ybit-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20680%20385'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="a pie chart showing the percentage of access for sale" title="Types of access" width="680" height="385" data-lazy-src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x385/10new1024x579-1739858016559.png"><noscript><img style="width:100% !important" loading="lazy" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x385/10new1024x579-1739858016559.png" alt="a pie chart showing the percentage of access for sale" title="Types of access" width="680" height="385"></noscript><button class="MuiButtonBase-root MuiIconButton-root MuiIconButton-sizeMedium ent-tss-yjupvi-expandIcon ent-tss-45-expandIcon-ref ent-css-1yxmbwk" tabindex="0" type="button"><svg class="MuiSvgIcon-root MuiSvgIcon-fontSizeMedium ent-css-vubbuv" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewbox="0 0 24 24" data-testid="OpenInFullIcon"><path d="M21 11V3h-8l3.29 3.29-10 10L3 13v8h8l-3.29-3.29 10-10z"></path></svg></button></div></div> <div data-slate-spacer="true" style="height:0;color:transparent;outline:none;position:absolute"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0"></span></span></span></div></div><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">In many cases, remote access is being supplied through the ConnectWise and TeamViewer software which provide actors with RDP-like capabilities.</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Exotic combinations are also possible: one of the actors stated he has access through TeamViewer that is located on RDP of the company managing their [a victim companys] software.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-55ybit-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20680%20411'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="a screen shot of a red and white web page" title="A threat actor claims he has access to a network from 400 computers via RDP" width="680" height="411" data-lazy-src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x411/11new1024x618-1739858056573.png"><noscript><img style="width:100% !important" loading="lazy" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x411/11new1024x618-1739858056573.png" alt="a screen shot of a red and white web page" title="A threat actor claims he has access to a network from 400 computers via RDP" width="680" height="411"></noscript><button class="MuiButtonBase-root MuiIconButton-root MuiIconButton-sizeMedium ent-tss-yjupvi-expandIcon ent-tss-45-expandIcon-ref ent-css-1yxmbwk" tabindex="0" type="button"><svg class="MuiSvgIcon-root MuiSvgIcon-fontSizeMedium ent-css-vubbuv" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewbox="0 0 24 24" data-testid="OpenInFullIcon"><path d="M21 11V3h-8l3.29 3.29-10 10L3 13v8h8l-3.29-3.29 10-10z"></path></svg></button></div></div> <div data-slate-spacer="true" style="height:0;color:transparent;outline:none;position:absolute"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0"></span></span></span></div></div><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:center"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">A threat actor claims he has access to a network from 400 computers via RDP; the screenshot he shared indicates that ConnectWise is being used to provide access.</span></em></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Untraditional accesses are continuing to appear on sale  as</span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://kelacyber.com/the-initial-access-brokers-toolbox-remote-monitoring-and-management/" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">in the story of the RMM solution being compromised</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">, described by KELA in September 2020. Back then, we found out that it was ManageEngine Desktop Central developed by Zoho corporation that was targeted. In fact, this threat actor named pshmm is continuing to compromise the Desktop Central solution of different organizations. He is one of the most active actors in Q4 2020 who offered 18 accesses on sale continuing to exploit the software.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">KELA spotted another untraditional type of access  the DX NetOps Spectrum access to a company operating an airport, offered by another actor. It apparently refers to the DX NetOps and DX Spectrum network monitoring and fault management software. Since the software helps to manage IT infrastructure, it can probably be used to compromise the whole network even with lateral movement. The access was sold within 4 days of its listing. Considering the recent news about SolarWinds network management solutions being used in a global cyber espionage campaign, these offers seem to pose a serious threat to multiple organizations.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Interestingly, in some offers the actors did not mention the actual access supplied but specified the type of servers the access leads into. For example, a prominent threat actor offered ESX ROOT access which appears to relate to virtual servers deployed through VMware ESXi hypervisor software. However, its only the asset type and not the type of access, which can be provided through RDP, VPN and other means. The actor also specified for buyers CPU, RAM, storage, etc. It seems that such server details enable actors to understand if they can use compromised resources for crypto mining and other malicious activity.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-55ybit-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20680%20496'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="a screen shot of a web page with a lot of information" title="The examples highlight that cybercriminals, namely initial access brokers" width="680" height="496" data-lazy-src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x496/12new21024x746-1739858127315.png"><noscript><img style="width:100% !important" loading="lazy" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" namely initial access brokers" width="680" height="496"></noscript><button class="MuiButtonBase-root MuiIconButton-root MuiIconButton-sizeMedium ent-tss-yjupvi-expandIcon ent-tss-45-expandIcon-ref data-slate-length="0"></span></span></span></div></div><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">The examples highlight that cybercriminals, namely initial access brokers, continue to evolve and develop their TTPs, meaning that more types of software are targeted by attackers standing at the beginning of the ransomware infection chain.</span></strong></span></span></p><h2 class="ent-tss-7785c4-h2" color="inherit" id="some-notable-and-pricey-examples"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Some Notable (and Pricey) Examples</span></span></span></h2><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Lets take a look at the most expensive accesses and suggest what made them valuable.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">1. One actors offers included: Australian, Asian and US companies</span></strong></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Five of the most expensive accesses of Q4 2020 were offered by a single actor. Two offers  for a US IT company and another IT firm  were related to access through ConnectWise. They cost 5 BTC and $30,000, respectively.</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">In both offers, the actors specified that IT firms have a lot of clients, probably implying that their networks can be breached too, which made the accesses valuable. Its just another proof of a continuous and rising threat to MSPs  such offers are in demand in underground communities.</span></strong></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-55ybit-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20680%20272'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="An actor offers to buy access to MSPs making IT-outsourcing, hosting their clients and working with cloud solutions." title="An actor offers to buy access to MSPs making IT-outsourcing, hosting their clients and working with cloud solutions." width="680" height="272" data-lazy-src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x272/1151024x409-1739858185772.png"><noscript><img style="width:100% !important" loading="lazy" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x272/1151024x409-1739858185772.png" alt="An actor offers to buy access to MSPs making IT-outsourcing, hosting their clients and working with cloud solutions." title="An actor offers to buy access to MSPs making IT-outsourcing, hosting their clients and working with cloud solutions." width="680" height="272"></noscript><button class="MuiButtonBase-root MuiIconButton-root MuiIconButton-sizeMedium ent-tss-yjupvi-expandIcon ent-tss-45-expandIcon-ref ent-css-1yxmbwk" tabindex="0" type="button"><svg class="MuiSvgIcon-root MuiSvgIcon-fontSizeMedium ent-css-vubbuv" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewbox="0 0 24 24" data-testid="OpenInFullIcon"><path d="M21 11V3h-8l3.29 3.29-10 10L3 13v8h8l-3.29-3.29 10-10z"></path></svg></button></div></div> <div data-slate-spacer="true" style="height:0;color:transparent;outline:none;position:absolute"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0"></span></span></span></div></div><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:center"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">An actor offers to buy access to MSPs making IT-outsourcing, hosting their clients and working with cloud solutions.</span></em></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">The rest of the offers featured an Australian organization, a telecommunications company from Asia, and access to 700 companies through a compromised Asian internet providers network. For all of them, the type was stated as SW, suggesting that access is gained through some software. In two cases, the actor also states buyers will receive access to SSH servers and backups, or to SSH console, switches and routers. Final prices for offers range from 4 BTC to 7 BTC.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-55ybit-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20680%20403'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="a computer monitor and a tablet with a russian language message" title="Access to multiple companies through an Asian internet provider" width="680" height="403" data-lazy-src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x403/12new1024x606-1739858231883.png"><noscript><img style="width:100% !important" loading="lazy" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x403/12new1024x606-1739858231883.png" alt="a computer monitor and a tablet with a russian language message" title="Access to multiple companies through an Asian internet provider" width="680" height="403"></noscript><button class="MuiButtonBase-root MuiIconButton-root MuiIconButton-sizeMedium ent-tss-yjupvi-expandIcon ent-tss-45-expandIcon-ref ent-css-1yxmbwk" tabindex="0" type="button"><svg class="MuiSvgIcon-root MuiSvgIcon-fontSizeMedium ent-css-vubbuv" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewbox="0 0 24 24" data-testid="OpenInFullIcon"><path d="M21 11V3h-8l3.29 3.29-10 10L3 13v8h8l-3.29-3.29 10-10z"></path></svg></button></div></div> <div data-slate-spacer="true" style="height:0;color:transparent;outline:none;position:absolute"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0"></span></span></span></div></div><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:center"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">Access to multiple companies through an Asian internet provider</span></em></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">2. Mexican government body</span></strong></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">At the end of November 2020, a threat actor advertised access to Mexicos National Insurance and Surety Commission on two forums. On one forum, he began a 48-hour auction for the access starting at $70,000, and a buy now option of $100,000. On another forum, he offered to sell the access for the same $100,000. On December 4, 2020, the LockBit ransomware blog claimed to compromise the affected entity and demanded $1 million ransom, indicating the ransomware group may have purchased the access from the threat actor.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">The actor himself</span></strong></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://noticiasseguridad.com/hacking-incidentes/hacker-vende-acceso-a-448-servidores-de-la-comision-nacional-de-seguros-y-fianzas-de-mexico-cnsf-fuga-de-datos-de-millones-de-mexicanos/" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">contacted a local press</span></strong></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">trying to attract attention and force the victim to negotiate, suggesting that he finally sold the access for a share of the ransom or that he himself encrypted the network as LockBits affiliate.</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">This actor has already engaged with Lockbit in the past  in August 2020, he published in their thread a name of the victim with the aim to name-and-shame. When LockBits started their blog a little later, the victim appeared there as well. Moreover, recently he tried to enter Sodinokibis affiliate program.</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Such activity shows that some of the initial access brokers intend to graduate into affiliates, chasing bigger profits and a steady place in the ransomware ecosystem.</span></strong></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">3. US government body.</span></strong></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Access to a Texas government network was offered by a known threat actor with a good reputation and a confirmed history of sales who most probably switched to making deals in private. His October offer was among the most expensive ones  $35,000 for domain admin access. While many ransomware affiliates/operators claim they do not work with government networks, it can still be considered prospective access, especially with such high privileges. The offer was marked as sold on the same day.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-55ybit-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20680%20421'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="US government body." title="US government body." width="680" height="421" data-lazy-src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x421/161024x633-1739858288735.png"><noscript><img style="width:100% !important" loading="lazy" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x421/161024x633-1739858288735.png" alt="US government body." title="US government body." width="680" height="421"></noscript><button class="MuiButtonBase-root MuiIconButton-root MuiIconButton-sizeMedium ent-tss-yjupvi-expandIcon ent-tss-45-expandIcon-ref ent-css-1yxmbwk" tabindex="0" type="button"><svg class="MuiSvgIcon-root MuiSvgIcon-fontSizeMedium ent-css-vubbuv" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewbox="0 0 24 24" data-testid="OpenInFullIcon"><path d="M21 11V3h-8l3.29 3.29-10 10L3 13v8h8l-3.29-3.29 10-10z"></path></svg></button></div></div> <div data-slate-spacer="true" style="height:0;color:transparent;outline:none;position:absolute"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0"></span></span></span></div></div><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">4. Panasonic India</span></strong></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">An actor claimed to have VPN access to a company that manufactures electronic products owned by every fifth person in the world. Based on the companys revenue he shared, it narrowed the circle up to several major players in the electronic goods market. The mystery was solved two weeks later when he opened another thread and admitted that the access was found and burned by security researchers. The actor claimed the breached company was Panasonic India and that he managed to steal some corporate data for which he asks a $500,000 ransom. Apparently, Panasonic decided not to react to this statement since</span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://www.bankinfosecurity.com/panasonic-india-held-to-500k-ransom-data-released-a-15573" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span European oil and gas company.</span></strong></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">The RDP access for a European oil and gas company was offered by a new threat actor under different handles. data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">A new sale under another handle but with the same contact in Jabber</span></em></span></span></p><h2 class="ent-tss-7785c4-h2" color="inherit" id="top-initial-access-brokers"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Top Initial Access Brokers</span></span></span></h2><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">When monitoring activities of initial access brokers, its crucial to identify the most prominent players to better understand their TTPs in order to proactively defend organizations. KELA shares the top 5 most active initial access brokers and their TTPs from Q4. Each one of the actors offered more than 10 accesses for sale.</span></span></span></p><ol data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-pw0qn0-numberedList"><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Crasty.</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Active in Russian-speaking forums for several months, the actor started to trade in network accesses just recently. He mainly offers Citrix/RDWeb accesses to Australian, French, US organizations, as well as companies from other countries, many of them are universities.</span></span></span></li><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">pshmm.</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">A known actor primarily selling RMM accesses continues to compromise mostly US companies, supposedly using Zohos ManageEngine Desktop Central. Zohos investigation, conducted following KELAs research, suggests that the actor uses weak credentials to gain access.</span></span></span></li><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">drumrlu / 3lv4n.</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">The actor, active since July, continues to provide his customers with multiple accesses. In addition to his usual thread with accesses, he started a new topic where he claimed to compromise the VMware ESXi software of affected organizations.</span></span></span></li><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Barf.</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">The actor started offering accesses in December 2020, before that he was mostly trading dedicated servers. He usually sells RDP type of access with user privileges to companies from France, US, Brazil, Spain, Italy, and Germany.</span></span></span></li><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">7h0rf1nn.</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">The actor mostly offers RCE and webshells in the networks of compromised companies from education, telecommunications, financial, insurance and sports sectors.</span></span></span></li></ol><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Tracking initial access brokers public activity on underground forums is crucial for network defenders willing to understand the threat landscape and prevent damaging cyber-attacks. Recent incidents with</span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://kelacyber.com/easy-way-in-5-ransomware-victims-had-their-pulse-secure-vpn-credentials-leaked/" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Pulse Secure</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">and</span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://www.bleepingcomputer.com/news/security/passwords-exposed-for-almost-50-000-vulnerable-fortinet-vpns/" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Fortinet</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">VPNs credentials being exposed and later used by threat actors is only one example of threats that can be caught by defenders while monitoring darknet sources.</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Continually monitoring such activity, patching the vulnerable software and educating employees, is an approach that should be taken into service by all organizations that want to avoid the post-factum negotiations with the ransomware operators.</span></strong></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0"></span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://www.kelacyber.com/blog/1-million-is-just-the-beginning-q4-2020-in-network-access-sales/#_ftnref1" rel="follow" target="_self" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">[1]</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"></span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">*Two items werent included in the analysis. First item was a sale of more than 500 .gov and .edu networks that were first offered on auction for at least 25 BTC; then, the price lowered to 10 BTC. After a month, the sale was closed due to irrelevance. Second item was a sale of access to technological production of electronics of a Chinese company offered first for 150 BTC and then for 75 BTC. Reactions from other users in the thread were mostly stating the access is overpriced and after two weeks the actor stated its still relevant; most possibly, he didnt manage to sell it after all. Since such high prices would influence numbers in the analysis significantly, though the sales appeared to be unsuccessful, KELA chose to leave these offers out of the analysis.</span></em></span></span></p></div></div></div></article><div class="ent-tss-1uoc0jk-popularQuestionsWrapper"><div class="ent-tss-165re9h-titleWrapper"><h2 class="MuiTypography-root !important"},".en-from-the-blog-post-details":{"paddingTop":"0px"}}}},"defaultLocale":"en","host":"kelacyber.com","name":"kelacyber","section":"main","url":"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.kelacyber.com","appName":"kelacyber","widgetsWebsite":"kelacyber","website":"67583e08a0365cb3a8090712"},"home":{"userLoaded":false,"user":null,"showLoginDialog":false,"showLoginDialogBlocking":false,"userSubscribed":false,"loaded":false,"loadedKeepshoppers":false,"handleSubscribeClick":null,"recentQuestions":[],"comparisons":[],"blogPosts":[],"bottomBlogPosts":[],"featuredPosts":[],"productReviews":[],"targetedPosts":[],"top10Pages":[],"recommendedPosts":[],"products":[],"recentBlogPosts":[],"productComparisonCategories":[],"productComparisons":[],"productReviewCategories":[]},"ads":{"enabled":false,"toolbarStickyOffset":null,"fromTheBlogInFeed":{"desktop":{"slot":"","layoutKey":"","format":""},"mobile":{"slot":"","layoutKey":"","format":""}},"toolbarHeight":null,"client":null,"slot":{"list":null,"sticky":null,"square":null,"horizontal":null},"topic":{"mobile":{"format":null,"layoutKey":null,"slot":null},"desktop":{"format":null,"layoutKey":null,"slot":null}},"category":{"mobile":{"format":null,"layoutKey":null,"slot":null},"desktop":{"format":null,"layoutKey":null,"slot":null}},"productList":{"mobile":{"format":null,"layoutKey":null,"slot":null},"desktop":{"format":null,"layoutKey":null,"slot":null}}},"pages":{"pageData":null,"loaded":false},"learningCenter":{"post":null,"postLoaded":false},"forum":{"postLoaded":false,"post":null,"categoryLoaded":false,"category":null,"categoriesLoaded":false,"categories":[],"sideQuestions":[],"sideQuestionsLoaded":false,"tags":[],"tagsLoaded":false,"questionLoaded":false,"question":null,"questions":[],"questionsLoaded":false,"questionsPageSize":10,"questionsTotalPages":null,"totalQuestions":null,"mainPageCategorySlug":null,"mainPagePage":1,"popularTags":[]},"editorial":{"policy":null,"loadContributor":false,"writerBio":null,"contentPrinciples":null,"edito pie chart showing the percentage of initial access for sale","title":"$1 Million is Just the Beginning: Q4 2020 in Network Access Sales","caption":"a pie chart showing the percentage of initial access for sale"},"previewVideo":null,"locale":"en","variant":"default","brief":null,"briefRichText":null,"showTOC":true,"showCoverImage":true,"title":"$1 Million is Just the Beginning: Q4 2020 in Network Access Sales","richTitle":null,"subtitle":null,"richSubtitle":null,"text":"Multiple initial network accesses continue to appear for sale in underground forums every day, partially becoming an initial entry point for ransomware operators. Following KELAs analysis ofinitial access brokers activities in September 2020, weve assessed the listings of network access from all of Q4 2020.\nWeve shared some of the major takeaways below:\nKELA traced almost 250 initial network accesses listed for sale in Q4 2020. The cumulative price requested for all accesses surpasses $1.2 million. On average, we observed around 80 accesses offered for sale in each month of Q4 2020.Out of these network access listings,KELA found that at least 14% were noted as sold by actors.As the overall month-to-month number is lower than in September (108 accesses),KELA identified a growing trend of accesses being sold in private conversations rather than publicly in forums, likely the cause for the slight decline.While establishing a list of the most expensive accesses and the TTPs of their sellers, KELA discovered that the attack surface is constantly expanding, with initial access brokers offering new access types.Meanwhile, RDP- and VPN-based accesses, as well as vulnerabilities (allowing to run code using a specific flaw and potentially enabling actors to pivot further within the targeted environment), constitute the majority of the offers.\n\nSupply - Q4 2020\nKELA observed 242 accesses on sale in Q4 2020 on three underground forums  offered for a sum of $1,209,880*.[1] Its important to note that 24% of offers didnt specify the price. As well explain more later on, many accesses are being traded in private conversations, meaning this sum does not reflect the higher actual revenue of the initial access market.\nThe average price for access has grown from $4,960 in September to $6,684 in Q4 2020, while the median price lowered from $2,000 to $1,500. This means that while high prices for selected offers significantly influence the average price, a typical access on sale still costs around $1,500-2,000. For such a sum, threat actors usually offer domain admin type of access to medium-sized companies with hundreds of employees.\n\n\nTypical accesses offered for $1500-2000\nKELA can confirm that at least 14% of initial network accesses were noted as sold by the actors selling them  amounting to a sum of $133,900.This percentage has lowered since September (23%), but this metric depends only on brokers and their will to specify sold accesses or not. Therefore, it doesnt indicate that their conversion rate suddenly drastically changed; more likely, new actors who dont mark successful sales emerged on the market.\nOperations Going Quiet\nAn average number of accesses on sale for one month in Q4 is 25% less than in September 2020.However, it doesnt necessarily mean that initial access brokers suspended their activity. More possibly, they simply moved part of the deals in private conversations with middlemen or ransomware affiliates, in an effort to avoid detection from researchers.\nThis hypothesis is based on the fact that many initial access brokers commonly write to contact them privately in order to receive information about other accesses that theyre willing to sell. While such behavior always existed, there is a more recent trend that emerged these past couple of months  brokers often offer a bunch of accesses in one thread and request from potential buyers to contact them privately to get the whole list. Some of them are looking for one buyer and state that theyre ready to work for a percentage, most likely meaning a share from the amount gained in a successful ransomware attack.\n\nA threat actor claims he has more accesses than offered in his thread.\n\nThreat actors looking for buyers for multiple accesses. The left post offers 70 Citrix accesses from Tier 1 countries, while the right posts author claims to have constant supply of domain admin accesses that he is ready to sell to one buyer.\nIn Q4 2020, the core of the most active initial access brokers consisted only of around 10 actors.However, it doesnt mean that only their offers are valid and pose a significant threat. It seems that some prominent brokers moved their operations into private long-term deals with known threat actors. Some of these buyers can be actors that have dedicated threads on forums with titles such as Ill buy accesses, Buy networks, Buy Network Access to Corporations. Occasionally, such brokers in quiet-mode come back with pricey and valuable offers that they probably didnt succeed to sell.\n\nFor example, a well-known actor who was quite active during the summer almost completely stopped sharing his offers publicly during September and October. However, his one offer posted in October 2020 was access to a government body for a price of $35,000 (see in Some Notable (and Pricey) Examples segment of this post). The next access he posted was only in December 2020  and it was a valuable offer as well  access to a US school district estimated at $20,000.\nAnother notorious actor made a comeback in late October after almost four months of radio-silence. He was the top actor offering the most expensive accesses in Q4, ranging from $30,000 to $135,000 (see in Some Notable (and Pricy) Examples segment of this post).\nWe can speculate that the actors who found regular buyers during the first months of their activity didnt succeed in selling some pricey accesses to them. Therefore, they went on a forum to try their luck with other users publicly. There are also other reasons for less accesses offered by the same actors  such as a time off (one of the actors was not active on the forum for several months), but we can only hypothesize regarding this matter.\n\nA threat actor stating he is ready to buy accesses in private and specifying conditions (looking for accesses to companies from the US with minimum $300 million revenue). Based on his offer, accesses will be used to deploy ransomware: the actor claims locking Windows and Linux OS, mentions communicating with victims through the admin panel, as well as having a blog.\n\nIt seems that theinitial network accessmarket is bigger than what we are observing in public conversations happening on underground forums. To understand the real scope of threats, its necessary to keep tracking notorious initial access brokers and their TTPs, engage with them regularly, and identify new types of threats that may emerge.\nFurther Diversification of Accesses on Sale: RMM and Network Monitoring Solutions\nEvery organizations attack surface is constantly expanding  initial access brokers are finding new attack vectors and ways to supply access to buyers. In some cases, they provide different types of accesses and even suggest making some malicious actions on behalf of the buyer, making their business more customer-oriented.\n\nA threat actor offers different access privileges and types to compromise network of one organization.\nThe most common offer is RDP- and VPN-based access, an RCE vulnerability, and access to various Citrix products (virtualization software, networking products and so on).RDP- and VPN-based access, as well as access to different software, is usually provided in the form of valid credentials. After accessing a compromised machine via such access, an intruder can move laterally and eventually can succeed in stealing sensitive information, executing commands and delivering malware. The RCE vulnerability type of initial access is usually limited to the ability to run code using a specific vulnerability, which allows actors to pivot further within the targeted environment.\nSuch RDP- and VPN-based accesses, as well as RCE flaws and access to other popular products, constitute 45% of the accesses on sale, making them a more traditional type of access.It is important to know that the actors specify a type of access only in half of the cases, while in the rest of the offers they just point out a level of privileges (admin or user, local or domain)  or simply give no details.\n\nIn many cases, remote access is being supplied through the ConnectWise and TeamViewer software which provide actors with RDP-like capabilities.Exotic combinations are also possible: one of the actors stated he has access through TeamViewer that is located on RDP of the company managing their [a victim companys] software.\n\nA threat actor claims he has access to a network from 400 computers via RDP; the screenshot he shared indicates that ConnectWise is being used to provide access.\nUntraditional accesses are continuing to appear on sale  asin the story of the RMM solution being compromised, described by KELA in September 2020. Back then, we found out that it was ManageEngine Desktop Central developed by Zoho corporation that was targeted. In fact, this threat actor named pshmm is continuing to compromise the Desktop Central solution of different organizations. He is one of the most active actors in Q4 2020 who offered 18 accesses on sale continuing to exploit the software.\nKELA spotted another untraditional type of access  the DX NetOps Spectrum access to a company operating an airport, offered by another actor. It apparently refers to the DX NetOps and DX Spectrum network monitoring and fault management software. Since the software helps to manage IT infrastructure, it can probably be used to compromise the whole network even with lateral movement. The access was sold within 4 days of its listing. Considering the recent news about SolarWinds network management solutions being used in a global cyber espionage campaign, these offers seem to pose a serious threat to multiple organizations.\nInterestingly, in some offers the actors did not mention the actual access supplied but specified the type of servers the access leads into. For example, a prominent threat actor offered ESX ROOT access which appears to relate to virtual servers deployed through VMware ESXi hypervisor software. However, its only the asset type and not the type of access, which can be provided through RDP, VPN and other means. The actor also specified for buyers CPU, RAM, storage, etc. It seems that such server details enable actors to understand if they can use compromised resources for crypto mining and other malicious activity.\n\nThe examples highlight that cybercriminals, namely initial access brokers, continue to evolve and develop their TTPs, meaning that more types of software are targeted by attackers standing at the beginning of the ransomware infection chain.\nSome Notable (and Pricey) Examples\nLets take a look at the most expensive accesses and suggest what made them valuable.\n1. One actors offers included: Australian, Asian and US companies\nFive of the most expensive accesses of Q4 2020 were offered by a single actor. Two offers  for a US IT company and another IT firm  were related to access through ConnectWise. They cost 5 BTC and $30,000, respectively.In both offers, the actors specified that IT firms have a lot of clients, probably implying that their networks can be breached too, which made the accesses valuable. Its just another proof of a continuous and rising threat to MSPs  such offers are in demand in underground communities.\n\nAn actor offers to buy access to MSPs making IT-outsourcing, hosting their clients and working with cloud solutions.\nThe rest of the offers featured an Australian organization, a telecommunications company from Asia, and access to 700 companies through a compromised Asian internet providers network. For all of them, the type was stated as SW, suggesting that access is gained through some software. In two cases, the actor also states buyers will receive access to SSH servers and backups, or to SSH console, switches and routers. Final prices for offers range from 4 BTC to 7 BTC.\n\nAccess to multiple companies through an Asian internet provider\n2. Mexican government body\nAt the end of November 2020, a threat actor advertised access to Mexicos National Insurance and Surety Commission on two forums. On one forum, he began a 48-hour auction for the access starting at $70,000, and a buy now option of $100,000. On another forum, he offered to sell the access for the same $100,000. On December 4, 2020, the LockBit ransomware blog claimed to compromise the affected entity and demanded $1 million ransom, indicating the ransomware group may have purchased the access from the threat actor.\nThe actor himselfcontacted a local presstrying to attract attention and force the victim to negotiate, suggesting that he finally sold the access for a share of the ransom or that he himself encrypted the network as LockBits affiliate.This actor has already engaged with Lockbit in the past  in August 2020, he published in their thread a name of the victim with the aim to name-and-shame. When LockBits started their blog a little later, the victim appeared there as well. Moreover, recently he tried to enter Sodinokibis affiliate program.Such activity shows that some of the initial access brokers intend to graduate into affiliates, chasing bigger profits and a steady place in the ransomware ecosystem.\n3. US government body.\nAccess to a Texas government network was offered by a known threat actor with a good reputation and a confirmed history of sales who most probably switched to making deals in private. His October offer was among the most expensive ones  $35,000 for domain admin access. While many ransomware affiliates\u002Foperators claim they do not work with government networks, it can still be considered prospective access, especially with such high privileges. The offer was marked as sold on the same day.\n\n4. Panasonic India\nAn actor claimed to have VPN access to a company that manufactures electronic products owned by every fifth person in the world. Based on the companys revenue he shared, it narrowed the circle up to several major players in the electronic goods market. The mystery was solved two weeks later when he opened another thread and admitted that the access was found and burned by security researchers. The actor claimed the breached company was Panasonic India and that he managed to steal some corporate data for which he asks a $500,000 ransom. Apparently, Panasonic decided not to react to this statement sincethe actor posted a stolen archive a few days later.\n\nAn actor offering access to electronic manufacturer for sale.\n\nThe same actor posting stolen data from the company and claiming its Panasonic India\n5. European oil and gas company.\nThe RDP access for a European oil and gas company was offered by a new threat actor under different handles. The price lowered from 2 BTC to 1 BTC and eventually the topic was closed without naming a reason. The seller claimed to have domain admin access to a company with big revenue, therefore had all factors for a high price formation.\n\nOriginal post under one handle\n\nA new sale under another handle but with the same contact in Jabber\nTop Initial Access Brokers\nWhen monitoring activities of initial access brokers, its crucial to identify the most prominent players to better understand their TTPs in order to proactively defend organizations. KELA shares the top 5 most active initial access brokers and their TTPs from Q4. Each one of the actors offered more than 10 accesses for sale.\nCrasty.Active in Russian-speaking forums for several months, the actor started to trade in network accesses just recently. He mainly offers Citrix\u002FRDWeb accesses to Australian, French, US organizations, as well as companies from other countries, many of them are universities.pshmm.A known actor primarily selling RMM accesses continues to compromise mostly US companies, supposedly using Zohos ManageEngine Desktop Central. Zohos investigation, conducted following KELAs research, suggests that the actor uses weak credentials to gain access.drumrlu \u002F 3lv4n.The actor, active since July, continues to provide his customers with multiple accesses. In addition to his usual thread with accesses, he started a new topic where he claimed to compromise the VMware ESXi software of affected organizations.Barf.The actor started offering accesses in December 2020, before that he was mostly trading dedicated servers. He usually sells RDP type of access with user privileges to companies from France, US, Brazil, Spain, Italy, and Germany.7h0rf1nn.The actor mostly offers RCE and webshells in the networks of compromised companies from education, telecommunications, financial, insurance and sports sectors.\nTracking initial access brokers public activity on underground forums is crucial for network defenders willing to understand the threat landscape and prevent damaging cyber-attacks. Recent incidents withPulse SecureandFortinetVPNs credentials being exposed and later used by threat actors is only one example of threats that can be caught by defenders while monitoring darknet sources.Continually monitoring such activity, patching the vulnerable software and educating employees, is an approach that should be taken into service by all organizations that want to avoid the post-factum negotiations with the ransomware operators.\n[1]*Two items werent included in the analysis. First item was a sale of more than 500 .gov and .edu networks that were first offered on auction for at least 25 BTC; then, the price lowered to 10 BTC. After a month, the sale was closed due to irrelevance. Second item was a sale of access to technological production of electronics of a Chinese company offered first for 150 BTC and then for 75 BTC. Reactions from other users in the thread were mostly stating the access is overpriced and after two weeks the actor stated its still relevant; most possibly, he didnt manage to sell it after all. Since such high prices would influence numbers in the analysis significantly, though the sales appeared to be unsuccessful, KELA chose to leave these offers out of the analysis.","richText":"[{\"type\":\"paragraph\",\"children\":[{\"text\":\"Multiple initial network accesses continue to appear for sale in underground forums every day, partially becoming an initial entry point for ransomware operators.             <script>

        // if getParameterByName function is not defined, define it
        if (typeof getParameterByName === 'undefined') {
            function getParameterByName(name, url) {
                if (!url) url = window.location.href;
                name = name.replace(/[\[\]]/g, "\\$&");
                var regex = new RegExp("[?&]" + name + "(=([^&#]*)|&|#|$)"),
                    results = regex.exec(url);
                if (!results) return null;
                if (!results[2]) return '';
                return decodeURIComponent(results[2].replace(/\+/g, " "));
            }
        }

        jQuery(document).ready(function($) {

            // First detect if _GET params are present.
            // We only need utm_source, utm_medium and utm_campaign
            var utm_source = getParameterByName('utm_source');
            var utm_medium = getParameterByName('utm_medium');
            var utm_campaign = getParameterByName('utm_campaign');
            var utm_content = getParameterByName('utm_content');
            var utm_term = getParameterByName('utm_term');

            // Next we will use Cookies.js to set cookies

            // If _GET params are present, set cookies
            if (utm_source) {
                Cookies.set('kela_utm_source', utm_source, { expires: 1, path: '/' });
            }
            if (utm_medium) {
                Cookies.set('kela_utm_medium', utm_medium, { expires: 1, path: '/' });
            }
            if (utm_campaign) {
                Cookies.set('kela_utm_campaign', utm_campaign, { expires: 1, path: '/' });
            }
            if (utm_content) {
                Cookies.set('kela_utm_content', utm_content, { expires: 1, path: '/' });
            }
            if (utm_term) {
                Cookies.set('kela_utm_term', utm_term, { expires: 1, path: '/' });
            }

            // Check if cookies are present
            if (Cookies.get('kela_utm_source')) {
                utm_source = Cookies.get('kela_utm_source');
            }
            if (Cookies.get('kela_utm_medium')) {
                utm_medium = Cookies.get('kela_utm_medium');
            }
            if (Cookies.get('kela_utm_campaign')) {
                utm_campaign = Cookies.get('kela_utm_campaign');
            }
            if (Cookies.get('kela_utm_content')) {
                utm_content = Cookies.get('kela_utm_content');
            }
            if (Cookies.get('kela_utm_term')) {
                utm_term = Cookies.get('kela_utm_term');
            }

            // Wait 4 seconds for the form to load
            setTimeout(function() {
                
                // Find if hidden fields exist, for example: .hs-utm_campaign input
                if ($(".hs-utm_source input").length) {
                    $(".hs-utm_source input").val(utm_source);
                }
                if ($(".hs-utm_medium input").length) {
                    $(".hs-utm_medium input").val(utm_medium);
                }   

                if ($(".hs-utm_campaign input").length) {
                    $(".hs-utm_campaign input").val(utm_campaign);
                }

                if ($(".hs-utm_content input").length) {
                    $(".hs-utm_content input").val(utm_content);
                }

                if ($(".hs-utm_term input").length) {
                    $(".hs-utm_term input").val(utm_term);
                }

                if ($(".gfield--type-text.utm_source input").length) {
                    $(".gfield--type-text.utm_source input").val(utm_source);
                }

                if ($(".gfield--type-text.utm_medium input").length) {
                    $(".gfield--type-text.utm_medium input").val(utm_medium);
                }

                if ($(".gfield--type-text.utm_campaign input").length) {
                    $(".gfield--type-text.utm_campaign input").val(utm_campaign);
                }

                if ($(".gfield--type-text.utm_content input").length) {
                    $(".gfield--type-text.utm_content input").val(utm_content);
                }

                if ($(".gfield--type-text.utm_term input").length) {
                    $(".gfield--type-text.utm_term input").val(utm_term);
                }
                
            }, 4000);

            
        });
    </script>
